## Chapter: Building Earth-like Planets: From Nebular Gas to Ocean Worlds

### Introduction

In the previous chapter, we explored the formation of dust clouds and the process of star formation. Now, we will delve into the next step in the evolution of these stars - the formation of planetary systems. This chapter will cover the various processes involved in the formation of planetary systems, from the early stages of planetary embryos to the final stages of planetary differentiation. We will also discuss the role of nebular gas in shaping the characteristics of these planetary systems.

The formation of planetary systems is a complex and dynamic process that has been studied extensively by astronomers and planetary scientists. It is a crucial step in the development of a planet, as it determines the physical and chemical properties of the planet. The formation of planetary systems is also closely linked to the formation of stars, as the two processes occur simultaneously.

In this chapter, we will explore the different types of planetary systems that can form, including terrestrial and gas giant planets. We will also discuss the factors that influence the formation of these systems, such as the composition of the nebular gas and the initial conditions of the star. Additionally, we will examine the role of migration and accretion in shaping the final structure of planetary systems.

Overall, this chapter aims to provide a comprehensive understanding of the formation of planetary systems and their role in the evolution of stars. By the end of this chapter, readers will have a deeper appreciation for the complex and intricate processes involved in the creation of Earth-like planets and the diverse range of planetary systems that can form. 


# Building Earth-like Planets: From Nebular Gas to Ocean Worlds

## Chapter 3: Planetary Systems

 3.1: Planetary Embryos

Planetary embryos are the earliest stages of planetary formation. They are small, rocky bodies that form from the debris of a collapsing molecular cloud. These embryos are typically less than 100 kilometers in diameter and are made up of a mixture of rock, metal, and ice. As the embryos continue to grow, they begin to differentiate and form distinct layers, similar to the layers found in the Earth's interior.

The formation of planetary embryos is a crucial step in the evolution of a planet. It is during this stage that the embryos begin to acquire their own unique characteristics, such as size, composition, and rotation. These characteristics are determined by the initial conditions of the star and the composition of the nebular gas.

### Subsection 3.1a: Formation of Planetary Embryos

The formation of planetary embryos is a complex process that involves the accretion of smaller particles and the growth of the embryo through collisions. As the embryo grows, it begins to attract more particles and debris, leading to its further growth and differentiation.

The composition of the nebular gas also plays a significant role in the formation of planetary embryos. The gas can vary in composition depending on the type of star and the initial conditions of the cloud. For example, a star with a higher metallicity will have a higher concentration of heavy elements in its nebular gas, which can lead to the formation of larger and more massive planetary embryos.

The formation of planetary embryos is also influenced by the presence of other embryos in the system. As the embryos continue to grow, they can interact with each other through gravitational interactions and collisions. This can lead to the formation of larger bodies, known as planetesimals, which can then continue to grow and form larger planets.

In summary, the formation of planetary embryos is a crucial step in the evolution of a planet. It is during this stage that the embryos begin to acquire their own unique characteristics and lay the foundation for the final structure of the planet. The composition of the nebular gas and the presence of other embryos play a significant role in shaping the characteristics of these embryos. 


# Building Earth-like Planets: From Nebular Gas to Ocean Worlds

## Chapter 3: Planetary Systems

 3.1: Planetary Embryos

Planetary embryos are the earliest stages of planetary formation. They are small, rocky bodies that form from the debris of a collapsing molecular cloud. These embryos are typically less than 100 kilometers in diameter and are made up of a mixture of rock, metal, and ice. As the embryos continue to grow, they begin to differentiate and form distinct layers, similar to the layers found in the Earth's interior.

The formation of planetary embryos is a crucial step in the evolution of a planet. It is during this stage that the embryos begin to acquire their own unique characteristics, such as size, composition, and rotation. These characteristics are determined by the initial conditions of the star and the composition of the nebular gas.

### Subsection 3.1a: Formation of Planetary Embryos

The formation of planetary embryos is a complex process that involves the accretion of smaller particles and the growth of the embryo through collisions. As the embryo grows, it begins to attract more particles and debris, leading to its further growth and differentiation.

The composition of the nebular gas also plays a significant role in the formation of planetary embryos. The gas can vary in composition depending on the type of star and the initial conditions of the cloud. For example, a star with a higher metallicity will have a higher concentration of heavy elements in its nebular gas, which can lead to the formation of larger and more massive planetary embryos.

The formation of planetary embryos is also influenced by the presence of other embryos in the system. As the embryos continue to grow, they can interact with each other through gravitational interactions and collisions. This can lead to the formation of larger bodies, known as planetesimals, which can then continue to grow and form larger planets.

### Subsection 3.1b: Growth and Differentiation of Planetary Embryos

As planetary embryos continue to grow, they begin to differentiate and form distinct layers. This process is known as differentiation and is driven by the differences in density and composition between the various layers. The core, mantle, and crust are the three main layers that form in a planetary embryo.

The core is the innermost layer and is typically made up of iron and nickel. It is the densest layer and is responsible for generating the planet's magnetic field. The mantle is the middle layer and is made up of silicate rocks. It is less dense than the core and is responsible for the planet's plate tectonics. The crust is the outermost layer and is made up of silicate rocks. It is the least dense layer and is responsible for the planet's surface features.

The differentiation of these layers is influenced by the composition of the nebular gas and the presence of other embryos in the system. For example, a higher concentration of heavy elements in the nebular gas can lead to a larger and more massive core, while the presence of other embryos can disrupt the differentiation process and lead to the formation of a more homogenous interior.

### Subsection 3.1c: Planetary Embryo Migration

In addition to the growth and differentiation of planetary embryos, another important process is their migration. Planetary embryos can migrate through the nebular gas and interact with other embryos, leading to the formation of larger bodies and the eventual formation of a planet.

The migration of planetary embryos is driven by various factors, including the composition of the nebular gas, the presence of other embryos, and the gravitational interactions between them. This process can lead to the formation of multiple planets in a single system, each with its own unique characteristics.

In conclusion, the formation of planetary embryos is a complex and dynamic process that plays a crucial role in the evolution of a planet. The growth, differentiation, and migration of these embryos are all influenced by various factors and can lead to the formation of diverse and Earth-like planets.

# Building Earth-like Planets: From Nebular Gas to Ocean Worlds

## Chapter 3: Planetary Systems




### Introduction

In the previous chapter, we explored the formation of the solar system and the role of the nebular hypothesis in shaping our understanding of planetary formation. In this chapter, we will delve deeper into the process of accretion, a crucial step in the formation of Earth-like planets.

Accretion is the process by which solid particles in a protoplanetary disk grow in size by sticking together. This process is driven by the gravitational attraction between particles, and it is the primary mechanism by which planets form. The accretion process is complex and involves a series of steps, each of which plays a crucial role in shaping the final planet.

In this chapter, we will explore the various aspects of accretion, including the initial conditions that lead to the formation of solid particles, the growth of these particles through collisions and mergers, and the final stages of accretion where these particles coalesce to form a planet. We will also discuss the role of accretion in the formation of the Earth and other terrestrial planets in our solar system.

Understanding the process of accretion is crucial for several reasons. First, it provides a framework for understanding the formation of Earth-like planets. By studying the accretion process, we can gain insights into the conditions that led to the formation of our own planet. Second, accretion plays a crucial role in the evolution of planetary systems. The accretion process can influence the size and composition of planets, as well as the distribution of planetary bodies in a system. Finally, studying accretion can help us understand the formation of other planetary systems beyond our own.

In the following sections, we will explore these topics in more detail, starting with the initial conditions that lead to the formation of solid particles in a protoplanetary disk.




### Section: 3.1 Growth of dust particles:

The growth of dust particles is a crucial step in the process of accretion. It is during this stage that small particles, typically less than a micrometer in size, begin to grow in size through the sticking together of smaller particles. This process is driven by the gravitational attraction between particles, and it is the primary mechanism by which larger bodies, such as planets, form.

#### 3.1a Dust coagulation

Dust coagulation is a key process in the growth of dust particles. It is the process by which small particles, typically less than a micrometer in size, stick together to form larger particles. This process is driven by the gravitational attraction between particles, and it is the primary mechanism by which larger bodies, such as planets, form.

The process of dust coagulation can be understood in terms of the van der Waals force. This force is a weak intermolecular force that arises due to the temporary dipole moment induced in a molecule by the electric field of another molecule. The van der Waals force is responsible for the weak attractive forces between molecules, and it plays a crucial role in the sticking together of dust particles.

The van der Waals force can be described by the following equation:

$$
F = \frac{A}{r^6}
$$

where $F$ is the force, $A$ is the Hamaker constant, and $r$ is the distance between the particles. The Hamaker constant is a measure of the strength of the van der Waals force, and it depends on the properties of the particles, such as their size and shape.

The van der Waals force is responsible for the sticking together of dust particles, which leads to the growth of larger particles. This process is known as coagulation, and it is a key step in the process of accretion.

In the next section, we will explore the role of dust coagulation in the formation of larger bodies, such as planets.

#### 3.1b Agglomeration growth

Agglomeration growth is another important process in the growth of dust particles. It is the process by which larger particles, typically larger than a micrometer in size, grow in size through the sticking together of smaller particles. This process is driven by the gravitational attraction between particles, and it is the primary mechanism by which larger bodies, such as planets, form.

The process of agglomeration growth can be understood in terms of the van der Waals force, similar to dust coagulation. However, in agglomeration growth, the larger particles have a stronger gravitational attraction to smaller particles, leading to their sticking together. This process is also influenced by the size and shape of the particles, as well as the presence of impurities or coatings on the particles.

The growth of dust particles through agglomeration can be described by the following equation:

$$
\frac{dN}{dt} = \frac{1}{2}N(N-1)\sigma v
$$

where $N$ is the number of particles, $\sigma$ is the surface area of the particles, and $v$ is the relative velocity between particles. This equation describes the rate of change of the number of particles with time, and it shows that the rate of agglomeration growth increases with the number of particles and their relative velocity.

The growth of dust particles through agglomeration is a crucial step in the process of accretion. It leads to the formation of larger bodies, such as planetesimals and planets, which are the building blocks of Earth-like planets. In the next section, we will explore the role of agglomeration growth in the formation of these larger bodies.

#### 3.1c Planetesimal formation

Planetesimal formation is a critical stage in the process of accretion. It is the process by which larger bodies, known as planetesimals, form from the agglomeration of dust particles. These planetesimals are the building blocks of Earth-like planets, and their formation is a key step in the evolution of our solar system.

The formation of planetesimals can be understood in terms of the agglomeration growth process described in the previous section. As dust particles grow in size through agglomeration, they eventually reach a critical size where their gravitational attraction to other particles becomes strong enough to overcome the random motions of the particles. This leads to the formation of larger bodies, known as planetesimals.

The formation of planetesimals can be described by the following equation:

$$
\frac{dN}{dt} = \frac{1}{2}N(N-1)\sigma v
$$

where $N$ is the number of particles, $\sigma$ is the surface area of the particles, and $v$ is the relative velocity between particles. This equation describes the rate of change of the number of particles with time, and it shows that the rate of planetesimal formation increases with the number of particles and their relative velocity.

The formation of planetesimals is a complex process that involves the interaction of many factors, including the size and shape of the particles, the presence of impurities or coatings on the particles, and the relative velocities between particles. However, the basic principle behind planetesimal formation is the same as that behind the formation of larger bodies, such as planets: the gravitational attraction between particles leads to their sticking together, forming larger and larger bodies.

In the next section, we will explore the role of planetesimals in the formation of Earth-like planets.

#### 3.1d Planetary embryos

Planetary embryos are the next stage in the evolution of planetesimals. They are larger bodies, typically several hundred kilometers in diameter, that form from the agglomeration of planetesimals. These embryos are the precursors of Earth-like planets, and their formation is a crucial step in the process of accretion.

The formation of planetary embryos can be understood in terms of the agglomeration growth process described in the previous sections. As planetesimals continue to grow in size through agglomeration, they eventually reach a critical size where their gravitational attraction to other particles becomes strong enough to overcome the random motions of the particles. This leads to the formation of larger bodies, known as planetary embryos.

The formation of planetary embryos can be described by the following equation:

$$
\frac{dN}{dt} = \frac{1}{2}N(N-1)\sigma v
$$

where $N$ is the number of particles, $\sigma$ is the surface area of the particles, and $v$ is the relative velocity between particles. This equation describes the rate of change of the number of particles with time, and it shows that the rate of planetary embryo formation increases with the number of particles and their relative velocity.

The formation of planetary embryos is a complex process that involves the interaction of many factors, including the size and shape of the particles, the presence of impurities or coatings on the particles, and the relative velocities between particles. However, the basic principle behind planetary embryo formation is the same as that behind the formation of larger bodies, such as planets: the gravitational attraction between particles leads to their sticking together, forming larger and larger bodies.

In the next section, we will explore the role of planetary embryos in the formation of Earth-like planets.

#### 3.1e Protostellar disk

The protostellar disk is a crucial stage in the formation of Earth-like planets. It is a rotating disk of gas and dust that forms around a newly formed star. The disk is typically several hundred thousand kilometers in diameter and can contain up to a million times the mass of the Earth. The protostellar disk is the birthplace of planetesimals and planetary embryos, and it plays a key role in the process of accretion.

The formation of a protostellar disk can be understood in terms of the collapse of a molecular cloud. A molecular cloud is a vast region of gas and dust in space, typically several light-years across. These clouds are the birthplaces of stars, and when they collapse under their own gravity, they can form a protostar. As the protostar continues to grow, it begins to spin faster and faster due to the conservation of angular momentum. This leads to the formation of a rotating disk around the protostar, known as the protostellar disk.

The protostellar disk is a dynamic and evolving system. As it rotates, it begins to flatten out due to the centrifugal force. This leads to the formation of a thin disk, with the protostar at its center. The disk is initially composed of gas and dust, but as it continues to rotate, the gas begins to settle towards the center due to its higher mass. This leads to the formation of a gaseous envelope around the protostar, with the dust particles settling towards the midplane of the disk.

The protostellar disk is a crucial stage in the formation of Earth-like planets. It is here that planetesimals and planetary embryos form, as the dust particles in the disk begin to stick together due to the gravitational attraction between them. As these bodies continue to grow, they eventually reach a critical size where their gravitational attraction becomes strong enough to overcome the random motions of the particles. This leads to the formation of larger bodies, known as planetary embryos, which are the precursors of Earth-like planets.

In the next section, we will explore the role of the protostellar disk in the formation of Earth-like planets in more detail.

#### 3.1f Planetary embryo growth

The growth of planetary embryos is a critical stage in the formation of Earth-like planets. As we have seen in the previous section, planetary embryos form from the agglomeration of dust particles in the protostellar disk. Once formed, these embryos continue to grow in size through the accretion of additional dust particles.

The growth of planetary embryos can be understood in terms of the agglomeration growth process described in the previous sections. As planetary embryos continue to grow in size, they eventually reach a critical size where their gravitational attraction to other particles becomes strong enough to overcome the random motions of the particles. This leads to the formation of larger bodies, known as planetary embryos.

The growth of planetary embryos can be described by the following equation:

$$
\frac{dN}{dt} = \frac{1}{2}N(N-1)\sigma v
$$

where $N$ is the number of particles, $\sigma$ is the surface area of the particles, and $v$ is the relative velocity between particles. This equation describes the rate of change of the number of particles with time, and it shows that the rate of planetary embryo growth increases with the number of particles and their relative velocity.

The growth of planetary embryos is a complex process that involves the interaction of many factors, including the size and shape of the particles, the presence of impurities or coatings on the particles, and the relative velocities between particles. However, the basic principle behind planetary embryo growth is the same as that behind the formation of larger bodies, such as planets: the gravitational attraction between particles leads to their sticking together, forming larger and larger bodies.

In the next section, we will explore the role of planetary embryos in the formation of Earth-like planets.

#### 3.1g Planetary embryo coalescence

The coalescence of planetary embryos is a crucial step in the formation of Earth-like planets. As planetary embryos continue to grow in size, they eventually reach a point where their gravitational attraction to each other becomes strong enough to overcome the random motions of the particles. This leads to the formation of larger bodies, known as planetary embryos.

The coalescence of planetary embryos can be understood in terms of the agglomeration growth process described in the previous sections. As planetary embryos continue to grow in size, they eventually reach a critical size where their gravitational attraction to other particles becomes strong enough to overcome the random motions of the particles. This leads to the formation of larger bodies, known as planetary embryos.

The coalescence of planetary embryos can be described by the following equation:

$$
\frac{dN}{dt} = \frac{1}{2}N(N-1)\sigma v
$$

where $N$ is the number of particles, $\sigma$ is the surface area of the particles, and $v$ is the relative velocity between particles. This equation describes the rate of change of the number of particles with time, and it shows that the rate of planetary embryo coalescence increases with the number of particles and their relative velocity.

The coalescence of planetary embryos is a complex process that involves the interaction of many factors, including the size and shape of the particles, the presence of impurities or coatings on the particles, and the relative velocities between particles. However, the basic principle behind planetary embryo coalescence is the same as that behind the formation of larger bodies, such as planets: the gravitational attraction between particles leads to their sticking together, forming larger and larger bodies.

In the next section, we will explore the role of planetary embryos in the formation of Earth-like planets.

#### 3.1h Planetary embryo differentiation

The differentiation of planetary embryos is a critical stage in the formation of Earth-like planets. As planetary embryos continue to grow in size, they begin to differentiate into distinct layers based on their chemical composition and physical properties. This process is driven by the gravitational forces within the embryo, which cause the denser materials to sink towards the center, while the less dense materials rise towards the surface.

The differentiation of planetary embryos can be understood in terms of the agglomeration growth process described in the previous sections. As planetary embryos continue to grow in size, they eventually reach a critical size where their gravitational attraction to other particles becomes strong enough to overcome the random motions of the particles. This leads to the formation of larger bodies, known as planetary embryos.

The differentiation of planetary embryos can be described by the following equation:

$$

### Section: 3.1 Growth of dust particles:

The growth of dust particles is a crucial step in the process of accretion. It is during this stage that small particles, typically less than a micrometer in size, begin to grow in size through the sticking together of smaller particles. This process is driven by the gravitational attraction between particles, and it is the primary mechanism by which larger bodies, such as planets, form.

#### 3.1a Dust coagulation

Dust coagulation is a key process in the growth of dust particles. It is the process by which small particles, typically less than a micrometer in size, stick together to form larger particles. This process is driven by the gravitational attraction between particles, and it is the primary mechanism by which larger bodies, such as planets, form.

The process of dust coagulation can be understood in terms of the van der Waals force. This force is a weak intermolecular force that arises due to the temporary dipole moment induced in a molecule by the electric field of another molecule. The van der Waals force is responsible for the weak attractive forces between molecules, and it plays a crucial role in the sticking together of dust particles.

The van der Waals force can be described by the following equation:

$$
F = \frac{A}{r^6}
$$

where $F$ is the force, $A$ is the Hamaker constant, and $r$ is the distance between the particles. The Hamaker constant is a measure of the strength of the van der Waals force, and it depends on the properties of the particles, such as their size and shape.

The van der Waals force is responsible for the sticking together of dust particles, which leads to the growth of larger particles. This process is known as coagulation, and it is a key step in the process of accretion.

#### 3.1b Dust growth barriers

Despite the importance of dust coagulation in the growth of dust particles, there are several barriers that can hinder this process. These barriers can prevent the formation of larger particles and thus slow down the process of accretion.

One such barrier is the presence of electric charges on the surface of dust particles. These charges can repel each other, preventing the particles from sticking together. This is known as the Coulomb barrier.

Another barrier is the presence of impurities on the surface of dust particles. These impurities can disrupt the van der Waals forces between particles, preventing them from sticking together. This is known as the impurity barrier.

Finally, the presence of a gas phase can also act as a barrier to dust coagulation. The gas phase can disrupt the van der Waals forces between particles, preventing them from sticking together. This is known as the gas phase barrier.

These barriers can significantly slow down the process of dust coagulation and thus the process of accretion. However, they can also be overcome under certain conditions. For example, the presence of a magnetic field can neutralize the electric charges on dust particles, reducing the Coulomb barrier. Similarly, high temperatures can increase the mobility of impurities, reducing the impurity barrier. Finally, the presence of a solid state can provide a surface for dust particles to stick together, reducing the gas phase barrier.

In conclusion, despite the presence of these barriers, dust coagulation remains a crucial process in the growth of dust particles and the formation of larger bodies. Understanding these barriers and how to overcome them is essential for studying the process of accretion and the formation of Earth-like planets.

#### 3.1c Dust growth models

The process of dust growth is a complex one, influenced by a variety of factors including the properties of the dust particles themselves, the surrounding environment, and the presence of barriers such as those discussed in the previous section. To better understand this process, scientists have developed a number of models that describe the growth of dust particles.

One such model is the Stokes' Law, which describes the growth of a spherical particle in a viscous medium. The law is given by the equation:

$$
\frac{dR}{dt} = \frac{1}{18}\frac{G\rho_s(2R)^2}{\mu}
$$

where $dR/dt$ is the rate of particle growth, $G$ is the gravitational constant, $\rho_s$ is the density of the solid particle, $R$ is the radius of the particle, $\mu$ is the dynamic viscosity of the medium, and $t$ is time. This law assumes that the particle is spherical and that the growth is dominated by collisions with particles of the same size.

Another model is the Saffman-Turner model, which describes the growth of a particle in a turbulent medium. The model is given by the equation:

$$
\frac{dR}{dt} = \frac{1}{3}\frac{G\rho_s(2R)^2}{\mu}
$$

where the parameters have the same meaning as in the Stokes' Law. This model assumes that the particle is spherical and that the growth is dominated by collisions with particles of the same size, but it also takes into account the effects of turbulence.

These models provide a useful framework for understanding the process of dust growth, but they also have their limitations. For example, they assume that the particles are spherical and that the growth is dominated by collisions with particles of the same size. In reality, dust particles can have a wide range of shapes and sizes, and the growth process can be influenced by a variety of factors.

Despite these limitations, these models have been instrumental in advancing our understanding of the process of dust growth. They have been used to study a variety of phenomena, from the formation of planets to the behavior of dust in the interstellar medium. As our understanding of these phenomena continues to evolve, so too will our models of dust growth.




### Section: 3.1 Growth of dust particles:

The growth of dust particles is a crucial step in the process of accretion. It is during this stage that small particles, typically less than a micrometer in size, begin to grow in size through the sticking together of smaller particles. This process is driven by the gravitational attraction between particles, and it is the primary mechanism by which larger bodies, such as planets, form.

#### 3.1a Dust coagulation

Dust coagulation is a key process in the growth of dust particles. It is the process by which small particles, typically less than a micrometer in size, stick together to form larger particles. This process is driven by the gravitational attraction between particles, and it is the primary mechanism by which larger bodies, such as planets, form.

The process of dust coagulation can be understood in terms of the van der Waals force. This force is a weak intermolecular force that arises due to the temporary dipole moment induced in a molecule by the electric field of another molecule. The van der Waals force is responsible for the weak attractive forces between molecules, and it plays a crucial role in the sticking together of dust particles.

The van der Waals force can be described by the following equation:

$$
F = \frac{A}{r^6}
$$

where $F$ is the force, $A$ is the Hamaker constant, and $r$ is the distance between the particles. The Hamaker constant is a measure of the strength of the van der Waals force, and it depends on the properties of the particles, such as their size and shape.

The van der Waals force is responsible for the sticking together of dust particles, which leads to the growth of larger particles. This process is known as coagulation, and it is a key step in the process of accretion.

#### 3.1b Dust growth barriers

Despite the importance of dust coagulation in the growth of dust particles, there are several barriers that can hinder this process. These barriers can prevent the formation of larger particles and thus impede the process of accretion.

One such barrier is the presence of electric charges on the dust particles. These charges can repel each other, preventing the particles from sticking together. This can be overcome by the presence of a conducting medium, such as a gas or liquid, which can neutralize the charges and allow the particles to coagulate.

Another barrier is the presence of impurities on the dust particles. These impurities can disrupt the van der Waals forces between particles, preventing them from sticking together. This can be overcome by the presence of a catalyst, which can promote the formation of stronger bonds between the particles.

Finally, the size and shape of the dust particles can also pose a barrier to coagulation. Smaller particles may be too small to overcome the gravitational forces acting on them, while irregularly shaped particles may not align properly for coagulation to occur. This can be overcome by the presence of a surfactant, which can stabilize the particles and promote their alignment for coagulation.

In conclusion, while dust coagulation is a crucial step in the growth of dust particles, several barriers can hinder this process. However, with the right conditions and the presence of certain catalysts and surfactants, these barriers can be overcome, allowing for the continued growth of dust particles and the eventual formation of larger bodies.

#### 3.1c Overcoming growth barriers

Overcoming the barriers to dust coagulation is crucial for the process of accretion. As we have seen, these barriers can be due to electric charges, impurities, size and shape of particles, and the presence of a conducting medium. In this section, we will discuss some strategies for overcoming these barriers.

##### Overcoming Electric Charges

The presence of electric charges on dust particles can be a significant barrier to coagulation. However, this barrier can be overcome by the presence of a conducting medium. The conducting medium can neutralize the charges on the particles, allowing them to stick together. This can be achieved by introducing a gas or liquid that can conduct electricity. For example, in the early stages of planetary formation, the presence of a gas atmosphere can help neutralize the charges on dust particles, facilitating coagulation.

##### Overcoming Impurities

Impurities on dust particles can disrupt the van der Waals forces between particles, preventing them from sticking together. However, this barrier can be overcome by the presence of a catalyst. A catalyst can promote the formation of stronger bonds between particles, overcoming the disruptive effects of impurities. For example, in the formation of the Earth, the presence of iron catalysts helped overcome the disruptive effects of impurities, allowing for the formation of larger particles.

##### Overcoming Size and Shape of Particles

The size and shape of dust particles can pose a barrier to coagulation. Smaller particles may be too small to overcome the gravitational forces acting on them, while irregularly shaped particles may not align properly for coagulation to occur. However, this barrier can be overcome by the presence of a surfactant. A surfactant can stabilize the particles and promote their alignment for coagulation. For example, in the formation of the Earth, the presence of surfactants helped overcome the size and shape barriers, allowing for the formation of larger particles.

##### Overcoming the Lack of a Conducting Medium

In some cases, the lack of a conducting medium can pose a barrier to coagulation. However, this barrier can be overcome by the presence of a catalyst. A catalyst can promote the formation of stronger bonds between particles, overcoming the disruptive effects of the lack of a conducting medium. For example, in the formation of the Earth, the presence of iron catalysts helped overcome the lack of a conducting medium, allowing for the formation of larger particles.

In conclusion, overcoming the barriers to dust coagulation is crucial for the process of accretion. By understanding these barriers and implementing strategies to overcome them, we can better understand the process of planetary formation.




### Section: 3.2 Turbulent circumstellar discs:

Turbulence plays a crucial role in the formation and evolution of circumstellar discs. Turbulent motions within these discs can significantly affect the accretion process, influencing the growth of dust particles and the overall structure of the disc.

#### 3.2a Turbulent motions in discs

Turbulent motions in circumstellar discs are driven by a variety of factors, including the gravitational pull of the central star, the pressure gradient in the gas, and the viscosity of the disc. These motions can be described by the Navier-Stokes equations, which describe the motion of fluid substances.

The Navier-Stokes equations can be written as:

$$
\rho \left( \frac{\partial \mathbf{v}}{\partial t} + \mathbf{v} \cdot \nabla \mathbf{v} \right) = -\nabla p + \mu \nabla^2 \mathbf{v} + \rho \mathbf{g}
$$

where $\rho$ is the density of the fluid, $\mathbf{v}$ is the velocity of the fluid, $p$ is the pressure, $\mu$ is the dynamic viscosity, and $\mathbf{g}$ is the gravitational acceleration.

In the context of circumstellar discs, the Navier-Stokes equations can be used to describe the turbulent motions of the gas. These motions can be highly complex, involving a wide range of scales and velocities. However, the equations can be simplified under certain assumptions, such as the assumption of a thin disc and the assumption of a constant viscosity.

The Navier-Stokes equations can also be used to describe the transport of angular momentum in the disc. This is particularly important in the context of accretion, as the transport of angular momentum can affect the rate at which material falls onto the central star.

In the next section, we will discuss the role of turbulence in the growth of dust particles in circumstellar discs.

#### 3.2b Turbulent viscosity

Turbulent viscosity is a key parameter in the study of circumstellar discs. It is a measure of the resistance to flow of the gas in the disc, and it plays a crucial role in the transport of angular momentum and the accretion process.

The concept of turbulent viscosity is closely related to the concept of turbulent kinetic energy. Turbulent kinetic energy is a measure of the random motion of the gas, and it is a key factor in the generation of turbulence. The rate of generation of turbulent kinetic energy can be expressed as:

$$
\frac{\partial}{\partial t} \left( \frac{1}{2} \rho v^2 \right) = -\nabla \cdot \left( \frac{1}{2} \rho \mathbf{v} v^2 \right) - \nabla \cdot \left( \frac{1}{2} \rho \mathbf{v} \mathbf{v} \cdot \nabla \mathbf{v} \right) + \rho \mathbf{v} \cdot \nabla \left( \frac{1}{2} v^2 \right) - #### 3.2c Turbulent mixing

Turbulent mixing is a critical process in the formation and evolution of circumstellar discs. It is the process by which turbulent motions within the disc cause the mixing of different elements and compounds, leading to the homogenization of the disc's composition.

The efficiency of turbulent mixing is determined by the turbulent viscosity, which is a measure of the resistance to flow of the gas in the disc. The higher the turbulent viscosity, the more efficient the mixing will be. This is because a higher turbulent viscosity means that the gas is more resistant to the forces acting on it, allowing it to move more freely and mix with other elements.

The efficiency of turbulent mixing can be quantified using the Reynolds number, a dimensionless quantity that describes the relative importance of inertial forces to viscous forces in a fluid. The Reynolds number is defined as:

$$
Re = \frac{\rho u L}{\mu}
$$

where $\rho$ is the density of the fluid, $u$ is the characteristic velocity, $L$ is the characteristic length, and $\mu$ is the dynamic viscosity.

When the Reynolds number is high, the flow is turbulent and the mixing is efficient. When the Reynolds number is low, the flow is laminar and the mixing is inefficient.

Turbulent mixing plays a crucial role in the formation of Earth-like planets. It is responsible for the homogenization of the disc's composition, which is necessary for the formation of a rocky planet like Earth. It also plays a key role in the accretion process, influencing the rate at which material falls onto the central star.

In the next section, we will discuss the role of turbulent mixing in the growth of dust particles in circumstellar discs.

#### 3.2d Turbulent accretion

Turbulent accretion is a key process in the formation of circumstellar discs and, ultimately, Earth-like planets. It is the process by which material from the disc is accreted onto the central star, driven by turbulent motions within the disc.

The efficiency of turbulent accretion is also determined by the turbulent viscosity. A higher turbulent viscosity means that the material is more resistant to the forces acting on it, allowing it to move more freely and accrete onto the central star.

The rate of turbulent accretion can be estimated using the Shakura-Sunyaev model, which describes the accretion rate onto a black hole. The model is given by:

$$
\dot{M} = \frac{\dot{M}_{\rm Edd} \alpha}{4\pi r^2}
$$

where $\dot{M}$ is the accretion rate, $\dot{M}_{\rm Edd}$ is the Eddington accretion rate, $\alpha$ is the Shakura-Sunyaev parameter, and $r$ is the radius.

The Shakura-Sunyaev parameter $\alpha$ is a dimensionless quantity that describes the efficiency of the accretion process. It is typically assumed to be in the range $0.1 \leq \alpha \leq 0.3$, although it can vary depending on the specific conditions in the disc.

Turbulent accretion plays a crucial role in the formation of Earth-like planets. It is responsible for the growth of the central star, which is necessary for the formation of a rocky planet like Earth. It also plays a key role in the formation of the disc's structure, influencing the distribution of material and the formation of spiral arms.

In the next section, we will discuss the role of turbulent accretion in the formation of Earth-like planets, focusing on the specific conditions that promote efficient accretion and the implications for planetary formation.

#### 3.3a Planetesimal formation

Planetesimal formation is a critical stage in the process of planetary formation. It is the process by which small, solid objects, known as planetesimals, are formed from the dust and gas in a circumstellar disc. These planetesimals are the building blocks of larger bodies, including Earth-like planets.

The formation of planetesimals is driven by the process of accretion, where small particles of dust and gas collide and stick together to form larger objects. This process is facilitated by the turbulent motions within the disc, which bring the particles into contact and provide the necessary energy for the collisions.

The efficiency of planetesimal formation is influenced by several factors, including the turbulent viscosity, the gas-to-dust ratio, and the temperature of the disc. The turbulent viscosity, as discussed in the previous section, plays a crucial role in the accretion process. A higher turbulent viscosity means that the particles are more resistant to the forces acting on them, allowing them to move more freely and accrete onto larger objects.

The gas-to-dust ratio also plays a significant role in planetesimal formation. A higher gas-to-dust ratio means that there is more gas to shield the dust from the destructive effects of ultraviolet radiation. This allows the dust to grow larger before it is destroyed, increasing the chances of planetesimal formation.

The temperature of the disc is another important factor. As the disc evolves, it cools and the temperature gradient becomes more radial. This can lead to the formation of a snow line, beyond which water and other volatile molecules condense and become more abundant. The presence of these volatiles can enhance the formation of planetesimals, as they provide additional material for the growing particles.

The formation of planetesimals is a complex process that is still not fully understood. However, recent studies have provided valuable insights into the mechanisms driving planetesimal formation. For example, the work of Krijt et al. (2017) has shown that the formation of planetesimals can be enhanced by the presence of a radial temperature gradient, as it allows for the growth of larger particles. Similarly, the work of Drazkowska et al. (2017) has demonstrated that the formation of planetesimals can be inhibited by the presence of a vertical temperature gradient, as it can lead to the evaporation of the growing particles.

In the next section, we will discuss the role of planetesimals in the formation of Earth-like planets, focusing on the specific conditions that promote their growth and the implications for planetary formation.

#### 3.3b Planetesimal growth

The growth of planetesimals is a crucial step in the process of planetary formation. It is during this phase that the small, solid objects formed in the previous stage begin to grow in size, eventually leading to the formation of larger bodies such as Earth-like planets.

The growth of planetesimals is driven by the same process of accretion that is responsible for their formation. As planetesimals move through the disc, they encounter smaller particles of dust and gas. If the relative velocity between the planetesimal and the smaller particle is low enough, the two objects can collide and stick together. This process is known as accretion.

The efficiency of planetesimal growth is influenced by several factors, including the turbulent viscosity, the gas-to-dust ratio, and the temperature of the disc. The turbulent viscosity, as discussed in the previous sections, plays a crucial role in the accretion process. A higher turbulent viscosity means that the planetesimals are more resistant to the forces acting on them, allowing them to move more freely and accrete onto larger objects.

The gas-to-dust ratio also plays a significant role in planetesimal growth. A higher gas-to-dust ratio means that there is more gas to shield the dust from the destructive effects of ultraviolet radiation. This allows the dust to grow larger before it is destroyed, increasing the chances of planetesimal growth.

The temperature of the disc is another important factor. As the disc evolves, it cools and the temperature gradient becomes more radial. This can lead to the formation of a snow line, beyond which water and other volatile molecules condense and become more abundant. The presence of these volatiles can enhance the growth of planetesimals, as they provide additional material for the growing objects.

The growth of planetesimals is a complex process that is still not fully understood. However, recent studies have provided valuable insights into the mechanisms driving planetesimal growth. For example, the work of Krijt et al. (2017) has shown that the growth of planetesimals can be enhanced by the presence of a radial temperature gradient, as it allows for the growth of larger particles. Similarly, the work of Drazkowska et al. (2017) has demonstrated that the growth of planetesimals can be inhibited by the presence of a vertical temperature gradient, as it can lead to the evaporation of the growing objects.

#### 3.3c Planetesimal dynamics

The dynamics of planetesimals is a critical aspect of planetary formation. It involves the study of the forces acting on planetesimals and how these forces influence their motion and growth.

The primary forces acting on planetesimals are gravitational and collisional forces. Gravitational forces are due to the attraction of the planetesimal to the central star and to other planetesimals. Collisional forces are due to the impact of smaller particles on the planetesimal.

The gravitational force acting on a planetesimal can be calculated using Newton's law of universal gravitation:

$$
F = G \frac{m_1 m_2}{r^2}
$$

where $F$ is the gravitational force, $G$ is the gravitational constant, $m_1$ and $m_2$ are the masses of the planetesimal and the central star or another planetesimal, and $r$ is the distance between the two objects.

Collisional forces are more complex to calculate due to the variety of factors that can influence the outcome of a collision, such as the relative velocity and size of the colliding objects, the material properties of the planetesimal, and the presence of an atmosphere or other medium.

The dynamics of planetesimals is also influenced by the turbulent viscosity of the disc, as discussed in the previous sections. A higher turbulent viscosity can enhance the growth of planetesimals by allowing them to move more freely and accrete onto larger objects. However, it can also increase the likelihood of collisions and the associated collisional forces.

The gas-to-dust ratio and the temperature of the disc also play a role in planetesimal dynamics. A higher gas-to-dust ratio can increase the shielding of dust from ultraviolet radiation, allowing for the growth of larger planetesimals. A radial temperature gradient can enhance the growth of planetesimals by allowing for the growth of larger particles. However, a vertical temperature gradient can lead to the evaporation of the growing planetesimals.

The dynamics of planetesimals is a complex and ongoing area of research. Future studies will continue to provide insights into the mechanisms driving planetesimal growth and the factors influencing their dynamics.

#### 3.3d Planetesimal collisions

Collisions between planetesimals are a fundamental aspect of planetary formation. They play a crucial role in the growth and evolution of planetesimals, influencing their size, composition, and orbital properties.

Collisions between planetesimals can be classified into two main types: accretional collisions and disruptive collisions. In accretional collisions, the impacting planetesimal is absorbed by the target planetesimal, leading to its growth. In disruptive collisions, the impacting planetesimal breaks up the target planetesimal, leading to the formation of smaller fragments.

The outcome of a collision between planetesimals depends on several factors, including the relative velocity and size of the colliding objects, the material properties of the planetesimals, and the presence of an atmosphere or other medium.

The relative velocity between two planetesimals can be calculated using the conservation of angular momentum:

$$
v = \sqrt{\frac{G (m_1 + m_2) r}{2 \tau}}
$$

where $v$ is the relative velocity, $G$ is the gravitational constant, $m_1$ and $m_2$ are the masses of the planetesimals, $r$ is the distance between the two objects, and $\tau$ is the time scale of the collision.

The size of the planetesimals can also influence the outcome of a collision. Larger planetesimals are more likely to survive disruptive collisions due to their greater mass and strength.

The material properties of the planetesimals, such as their strength and toughness, can also affect the outcome of a collision. Stronger and tougher materials are more likely to survive disruptive collisions.

The presence of an atmosphere or other medium can also influence the outcome of a collision. An atmosphere can provide a cushion for the colliding planetesimals, reducing the impact force. It can also increase the likelihood of accretional collisions by providing a medium for the impacting planetesimal to stick to the target planetesimal.

The study of planetesimal collisions is a complex and ongoing area of research. Future studies will continue to provide insights into the mechanisms driving planetary formation and the role of collisions in this process.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the complex and fascinating world of planetary formation. We have explored the various processes that govern the formation of planets, from the initial stages of planetesimal accretion to the final stages of differentiation and core formation. We have also examined the role of various factors such as temperature, pressure, and composition in shaping the characteristics of these planets.

We have seen how the nebular hypothesis, proposed by Laplace, provides a theoretical framework for understanding the formation of the solar system. We have also discussed the role of turbulence and magnetic fields in the formation of planets, and how these factors can influence the final outcome of the formation process.

Furthermore, we have examined the concept of planetary differentiation, where the internal structure of a planet becomes distinct due to variations in temperature and pressure. This process is crucial in the formation of the Earth as we know it today, and it is a key aspect of planetary formation in general.

In conclusion, the formation of planets is a complex and multifaceted process that involves a variety of physical and chemical processes. Understanding these processes is crucial for gaining insights into the origins of our planet and the rest of the solar system.

## Chapter 4: The Earth's Early Atmosphere

### Introduction

The Earth's early atmosphere is a fascinating and complex topic that has been studied extensively by scientists. This chapter will delve into the intricacies of the Earth's early atmosphere, exploring its composition, evolution, and the processes that shaped it.

The early atmosphere of the Earth is a critical component of the planet's history. It is the medium through which the Earth's surface has interacted with the rest of the universe since its formation. The early atmosphere was not just a passive backdrop; it played an active role in shaping the Earth's surface and the life that exists on it.

The composition of the early atmosphere is a subject of ongoing research. It is believed that the early atmosphere was primarily composed of gases released by volcanic activity, such as carbon dioxide, methane, and ammonia. However, the exact composition of the early atmosphere is still not fully understood.

The evolution of the early atmosphere is another intriguing aspect of this topic. The early atmosphere is thought to have undergone significant changes over time, influenced by factors such as the Earth's rotation, the presence of a magnetic field, and the interaction with other bodies in the solar system.

The processes that shaped the early atmosphere are also of great interest. These include the greenhouse effect, which helped to keep the early Earth warm, and the photolysis of molecules by ultraviolet light, which led to the formation of new compounds.

In this chapter, we will explore these topics in depth, providing a comprehensive overview of the Earth's early atmosphere. We will also discuss the implications of the early atmosphere for the development of life on Earth and the ongoing research in this field.

This chapter aims to provide a solid foundation for understanding the Earth's early atmosphere, equipping readers with the knowledge and tools to further explore this fascinating topic. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or simply someone with a keen interest in the history of our planet, we hope that this chapter will enhance your understanding of the Earth's early atmosphere.




#### 3.2d Turbulent accretion

Turbulent accretion is a critical process in the formation and evolution of circumstellar discs. It is the process by which material from the disc is accreted onto the central star, driven by turbulent motions within the disc.

The efficiency of turbulent accretion is determined by the turbulent viscosity, which is a measure of the resistance to flow of the gas in the disc. The higher the turbulent viscosity, the more efficient the accretion will be. This is because a higher turbulent viscosity means that the gas is more resistant to the forces acting on it, allowing it to move more freely and accrete onto the central star.

The efficiency of turbulent accretion can be quantified using the Reynolds number, a dimensionless quantity that describes the relative importance of inertial forces to viscous forces in a fluid. The Reynolds number is defined as:

$$
Re = \frac{\rho u L}{\mu}
$$

where $\rho$ is the density of the fluid, $u$ is the characteristic velocity, $L$ is the characteristic length, and $\mu$ is the dynamic viscosity.

When the Reynolds number is high, the flow is turbulent and the accretion is efficient. When the Reynolds number is low, the flow is laminar and the accretion is inefficient.

Turbulent accretion plays a crucial role in the formation of Earth-like planets. It is responsible for the growth of the central star, which is necessary for the formation of a rocky planet like Earth. It also plays a key role in the accretion process, influencing the rate at which material falls onto the central star.

In the next section, we will discuss the role of turbulent accretion in the formation of planetesimals, the building blocks of Earth-like planets.

#### 3.2e Turbulent fragmentation

Turbulent fragmentation is a key process in the formation of circumstellar discs and, ultimately, Earth-like planets. It is the process by which a turbulent disc breaks down into smaller fragments, leading to the formation of planetesimals and, eventually, planets.

The process of turbulent fragmentation is driven by the interaction of turbulent motions and gravitational forces within the disc. Turbulent motions can cause the disc to become unstable, leading to the formation of eddies and vortices. These eddies and vortices can then interact with the gravitational field of the central star, leading to the formation of spiral arms and, eventually, planetesimals.

The efficiency of turbulent fragmentation is determined by the turbulent viscosity, which is a measure of the resistance to flow of the gas in the disc. The higher the turbulent viscosity, the more efficient the fragmentation will be. This is because a higher turbulent viscosity means that the gas is more resistant to the forces acting on it, allowing it to move more freely and fragment into smaller pieces.

The efficiency of turbulent fragmentation can be quantified using the Reynolds number, a dimensionless quantity that describes the relative importance of inertial forces to viscous forces in a fluid. The Reynolds number is defined as:

$$
Re = \frac{\rho u L}{\mu}
$$

where $\rho$ is the density of the fluid, $u$ is the characteristic velocity, $L$ is the characteristic length, and $\mu$ is the dynamic viscosity.

When the Reynolds number is high, the flow is turbulent and the fragmentation is efficient. When the Reynolds number is low, the flow is laminar and the fragmentation is inefficient.

Turbulent fragmentation plays a crucial role in the formation of Earth-like planets. It is responsible for the formation of planetesimals, the building blocks of planets. These planetesimals can then collide and merge to form larger bodies, eventually leading to the formation of Earth-like planets.

In the next section, we will discuss the role of turbulent fragmentation in the formation of planetesimals and, eventually, Earth-like planets.

#### 3.2f Turbulent growth

Turbulent growth is a critical process in the formation of circumstellar discs and, ultimately, Earth-like planets. It is the process by which a turbulent disc grows in size and mass, leading to the formation of larger bodies such as planetesimals and, eventually, planets.

The process of turbulent growth is driven by the interaction of turbulent motions and gravitational forces within the disc. Turbulent motions can cause the disc to become unstable, leading to the formation of eddies and vortices. These eddies and vortices can then interact with the gravitational field of the central star, leading to the formation of spiral arms and, eventually, planetesimals.

The efficiency of turbulent growth is determined by the turbulent viscosity, which is a measure of the resistance to flow of the gas in the disc. The higher the turbulent viscosity, the more efficient the growth will be. This is because a higher turbulent viscosity means that the gas is more resistant to the forces acting on it, allowing it to move more freely and grow in size.

The efficiency of turbulent growth can be quantified using the Reynolds number, a dimensionless quantity that describes the relative importance of inertial forces to viscous forces in a fluid. The Reynolds number is defined as:

$$
Re = \frac{\rho u L}{\mu}
$$

where $\rho$ is the density of the fluid, $u$ is the characteristic velocity, $L$ is the characteristic length, and $\mu$ is the dynamic viscosity.

When the Reynolds number is high, the flow is turbulent and the growth is efficient. When the Reynolds number is low, the flow is laminar and the growth is inefficient.

Turbulent growth plays a crucial role in the formation of Earth-like planets. It is responsible for the formation of planetesimals, the building blocks of planets. These planetesimals can then collide and merge to form larger bodies, eventually leading to the formation of Earth-like planets.

In the next section, we will discuss the role of turbulent growth in the formation of planetesimals and, eventually, Earth-like planets.

#### 3.2g Turbulent accretion and planetesimal formation

Turbulent accretion is a key process in the formation of planetesimals and, ultimately, Earth-like planets. It is the process by which material from the turbulent disc is accreted onto planetesimals, leading to their growth and eventual formation.

The process of turbulent accretion is driven by the interaction of turbulent motions and gravitational forces within the disc. Turbulent motions can cause the disc to become unstable, leading to the formation of eddies and vortices. These eddies and vortices can then interact with the gravitational field of the central star, leading to the formation of spiral arms and, eventually, planetesimals.

The efficiency of turbulent accretion is determined by the turbulent viscosity, which is a measure of the resistance to flow of the gas in the disc. The higher the turbulent viscosity, the more efficient the accretion will be. This is because a higher turbulent viscosity means that the gas is more resistant to the forces acting on it, allowing it to move more freely and accrete onto planetesimals.

The efficiency of turbulent accretion can be quantified using the Reynolds number, a dimensionless quantity that describes the relative importance of inertial forces to viscous forces in a fluid. The Reynolds number is defined as:

$$
Re = \frac{\rho u L}{\mu}
$$

where $\rho$ is the density of the fluid, $u$ is the characteristic velocity, $L$ is the characteristic length, and $\mu$ is the dynamic viscosity.

When the Reynolds number is high, the flow is turbulent and the accretion is efficient. When the Reynolds number is low, the flow is laminar and the accretion is inefficient.

Turbulent accretion plays a crucial role in the formation of Earth-like planets. It is responsible for the formation of planetesimals, the building blocks of planets. These planetesimals can then collide and merge to form larger bodies, eventually leading to the formation of Earth-like planets.

In the next section, we will discuss the role of turbulent accretion in the formation of planetesimals and, eventually, Earth-like planets.

#### 3.2h Turbulent growth and planetesimal formation

Turbulent growth is another key process in the formation of planetesimals and, ultimately, Earth-like planets. It is the process by which planetesimals grow in size and mass through the accretion of material from the turbulent disc.

The process of turbulent growth is driven by the same turbulent motions and gravitational forces that drive turbulent accretion. As the disc becomes unstable due to these forces, eddies and vortices form, leading to the formation of spiral arms. These spiral arms can then interact with planetesimals, leading to their growth and eventual formation.

The efficiency of turbulent growth is also determined by the turbulent viscosity. A higher turbulent viscosity means that the gas is more resistant to the forces acting on it, allowing it to move more freely and accrete onto planetesimals. This results in more efficient growth.

The efficiency of turbulent growth can be quantified using the Reynolds number, as with turbulent accretion. When the Reynolds number is high, the flow is turbulent and the growth is efficient. When the Reynolds number is low, the flow is laminar and the growth is inefficient.

Turbulent growth plays a crucial role in the formation of Earth-like planets. It is responsible for the formation of planetesimals, the building blocks of planets. These planetesimals can then collide and merge to form larger bodies, eventually leading to the formation of Earth-like planets.

In the next section, we will discuss the role of turbulent growth in the formation of planetesimals and, eventually, Earth-like planets.

#### 3.2i Turbulent fragmentation and planetesimal formation

Turbulent fragmentation is a critical process in the formation of planetesimals and, ultimately, Earth-like planets. It is the process by which a turbulent disc breaks down into smaller fragments, leading to the formation of planetesimals.

The process of turbulent fragmentation is driven by the same turbulent motions and gravitational forces that drive turbulent accretion and growth. As the disc becomes unstable due to these forces, eddies and vortices form, leading to the formation of spiral arms. These spiral arms can then interact with each other, leading to the formation of larger structures known as planetesimals.

The efficiency of turbulent fragmentation is also determined by the turbulent viscosity. A higher turbulent viscosity means that the gas is more resistant to the forces acting on it, allowing it to move more freely and fragment into smaller pieces. This results in more efficient fragmentation.

The efficiency of turbulent fragmentation can be quantified using the Reynolds number, as with turbulent accretion and growth. When the Reynolds number is high, the flow is turbulent and the fragmentation is efficient. When the Reynolds number is low, the flow is laminar and the fragmentation is inefficient.

Turbulent fragmentation plays a crucial role in the formation of Earth-like planets. It is responsible for the formation of planetesimals, the building blocks of planets. These planetesimals can then collide and merge to form larger bodies, eventually leading to the formation of Earth-like planets.

In the next section, we will discuss the role of turbulent fragmentation in the formation of planetesimals and, eventually, Earth-like planets.

#### 3.2j Turbulent mixing and planetesimal formation

Turbulent mixing is a key process in the formation of planetesimals and, ultimately, Earth-like planets. It is the process by which material from different regions of a turbulent disc is mixed together, leading to the formation of planetesimals.

The process of turbulent mixing is driven by the same turbulent motions and gravitational forces that drive turbulent accretion, growth, and fragmentation. As the disc becomes unstable due to these forces, eddies and vortices form, leading to the formation of spiral arms. These spiral arms can then interact with each other, leading to the formation of larger structures known as planetesimals.

The efficiency of turbulent mixing is also determined by the turbulent viscosity. A higher turbulent viscosity means that the gas is more resistant to the forces acting on it, allowing it to move more freely and mix with other regions of the disc. This results in more efficient mixing.

The efficiency of turbulent mixing can be quantified using the Reynolds number, as with turbulent accretion, growth, and fragmentation. When the Reynolds number is high, the flow is turbulent and the mixing is efficient. When the Reynolds number is low, the flow is laminar and the mixing is inefficient.

Turbulent mixing plays a crucial role in the formation of Earth-like planets. It is responsible for the formation of planetesimals, the building blocks of planets. These planetesimals can then collide and merge to form larger bodies, eventually leading to the formation of Earth-like planets.

In the next section, we will discuss the role of turbulent mixing in the formation of planetesimals and, eventually, Earth-like planets.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the various processes involved in the formation of Earth-like planets. We have delved into the intricacies of accretion, a process that involves the growth of a planetary body by the accumulation of smaller particles. We have also examined the role of turbulence in the formation of circumstellar discs, which are the birthplaces of planets. 

We have also discussed the importance of planetesimals, the building blocks of planets, and how they contribute to the formation of Earth-like planets. The chapter has also highlighted the significance of the Kuiper Belt and the Oort Cloud in the formation of these planets. 

In conclusion, the formation of Earth-like planets is a complex process that involves a series of interconnected events. It is a process that is still not fully understood, but the knowledge we have gained so far is a significant step towards understanding the origins of our planet and others like it.
